<div id="review_237446184" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/237446184" />
    <a title="Geoffrey Fox" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3024716-geoffrey-fox"><img alt="Geoffrey Fox" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442467239p2/3024716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/237446184?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoffrey Fox" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoffrey Fox" href="/user/show/3024716-geoffrey-fox">Geoffrey Fox</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer237446184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1553226860959389162">The encounter and transforming love between Rumi and the wandering dervish Shams i-Tabriz in the 1240s is background, context and explanation of the transformation in 2008 and 2009 of Ella Rubinstein, Jewish American housewife in Northampton MA, through contact with their story. Shams turns the respected and sedate scholar Rumi into a poet and co-founder (along with Shams) of the whirling dervishes; their story turns Ella from a self-repressed, resigned wife in a loveless marriage into a free an</span>
  <span id="freeText1553226860959389162" style="display:none">The encounter and transforming love between Rumi and the wandering dervish Shams i-Tabriz in the 1240s is background, context and explanation of the transformation in 2008 and 2009 of Ella Rubinstein, Jewish American housewife in Northampton MA, through contact with their story. Shams turns the respected and sedate scholar Rumi into a poet and co-founder (along with Shams) of the whirling dervishes; their story turns Ella from a self-repressed, resigned wife in a loveless marriage into a free and adventurous woman. Alternating chapters are told from the points of view of Ella or Shams and the many people who come in contact directly with him in Konya, Damascus or Tabriz. His stern but gentle manner and his preachings of love arouse strong reactions, ranging from murderous hostility on the part of Islamic zealots to almost total identification by Rumi, from respect and devotion by outcastes whom he has consoled and aided to the one kind of love he cannot allow himself, the passionate, carnal kind. Which may be what you thought this book was going to be about, but no, Shams' 40 Rules of Love are Sufi rules, of accepting one's fate but aiding and preventing harm to others and trusting in God's overall just design of all things. The book is a welcome introduction to this moment in Sufism and the origins of the Mahlevi whirling dervishes ("Mahlev" or master was what Rumi was called), and the twin stories — of the 13th and of the 21st centuries — come to a satisfying conclusion. <br /><br />However, Shafak's narrative structure and voice here are so limited that one longs for a little break now and then. Each chapter tells us the thoughts and observations of just one character at a time, often telling us things that they would be unlikely to say even to themselves, and everybody sounds alike, whether a drunk or a prostitute or enlightened one in Konya in 1246 or Ella Rubinstein in 2008. The drunk tells us he is drunk but he doesn't sound drunk, the angry zealot tells us he is an angry zealot but doesn't sound very excited about it, and so on. “"Beauty is in the eye of the beholder," Shams kept saying.” (p. 268) He sounds just like Ella. These limitations are quite unlike Elif Shafak's approach in her earlier novel, The Bastard of Istanbul (see my review), where there are different voices and narrative points of view, including a genie and an Internet forum. But "40 Rules" comes to a good, perfectly Sufi ending, which goes far to compensate for other weaknesses, and in the course of reading it we learn much about why Sufism is so appealing to so many.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1553226860959389162" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_178902870" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178902870" />
    <a title="Karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/914730-karen"><img alt="Karen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178902870?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen" href="/user/show/914730-karen">Karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer178902870&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178902870" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11124618242204583567">I wanted to like this book, but couldn't, as hard as I tried. The writing felt forced, almost like a translation, but there was no mention of a translator, so I am guessing it was written in English. On a structural level, I felt the novel was disjointed - going back and forth in time between too many superficially developed characters. I was confused by the use of informal, modern-day colloquialisms in the 13th century voices - these expressions were so well-integrated into the novel that it se</span>
  <span id="freeText11124618242204583567" style="display:none">I wanted to like this book, but couldn't, as hard as I tried. The writing felt forced, almost like a translation, but there was no mention of a translator, so I am guessing it was written in English. On a structural level, I felt the novel was disjointed - going back and forth in time between too many superficially developed characters. I was confused by the use of informal, modern-day colloquialisms in the 13th century voices - these expressions were so well-integrated into the novel that it seems to have been a deliberate choice on the part of the writer, but it made the narration seem very unrealistic.<br /><br />On a deeper level, knowing nothing about Sufism, I finished the book feeling at best ambivalent and at worst antagonistic toward the Sufi characters and those influenced by Sufism. I tried to be open-minded, but Shams of Tabriz seemed arrogant, self-righteous, and self-absorbed (insufferable comes to mind)rather than ego-less and selfless, and Rumi's absolute indifference toward his younger son seemed to have contributed to the heartless choices Aladdin ended up making at the end. Additionally, I could not forgive Ella for abandoning her children, particularly her adolescent daughter with bulimia. What bothered me is that the writer seemed to be condoning and even glorifying these choices as being spiritually enlightened, in the name of love.<br /><br />In general, the book felt too weighted and serious, and needed more lightness and humor to be enjoyable.<br /><br />Also, it was just bizarre that the author suddenly injected information about Ella's father's suicide into the narrative, and only referred to it once more in the entire book. This kind of seems like an important part of the story, which deserves further exploration.<br /><br />However, there were some moments of compelling writing, and the book did get me interested in learning more about Sufism in particular and mysticism in general - as well as being more open to spirituality in everyday life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11124618242204583567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2628180575" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2628180575" />
    <a title="Prashasti " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13581884-prashasti"><img alt="Prashasti " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585201015p2/13581884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2628180575?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Prashasti " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Prashasti " href="/user/show/13581884-prashasti">Prashasti </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11525157-the-forty-rules-of-love">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13581884-prashasti?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2628180575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2951526947896099510"><b>
  <i>"Whatever you see as profitable, flee from it!<br/>Drink poison and pour away the water of life!<br/>Abandon security and stay in frightful places!<br/>Throw away reputation, become disgraced and shameless"</i>
</b><br/><br/>As a child, I recall being fascinated with Sufism, the twirling dervishes, spinning their bodies with their skirts and dishdashas flying in repetitive circles listening to the music and focusing on Allah, completely lost and devoted in Sema. <br/><br/>With that image in my mind also accompanied with the fact of my</span>
  <span id="freeText2951526947896099510" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"Whatever you see as profitable, flee from it!<br />Drink poison and pour away the water of life!<br />Abandon security and stay in frightful places!<br />Throw away reputation, become disgraced and shameless"</i>
</b><br /><br />As a child, I recall being fascinated with Sufism, the twirling dervishes, spinning their bodies with their skirts and dishdashas flying in repetitive circles listening to the music and focusing on Allah, completely lost and devoted in Sema. <br /><br />With that image in my mind also accompanied with the fact of myself being an ardent follower of Rumi's poetry...I wanted to read this book since a while but it's only now when I caught hold of it and set up my mind for this journey.<br /><br />There are two stories of relationships, one that takes place in the present between Ella and Aziz, and one that takes places in the past between Shams and Rumi. The problem with this book isn't that two different storylines are interlaced; it's that the present-day narrative is horrendously clunky in its execution that it jars against the rest of the book. <br />Ignoring the modern part of the story which plainly feels contrived into the book, the parts that take place in Konya are intriguing through the first half of the book but then the author gets sloppy, and Shams, loses all credibility as to the fault of the author, whom likely lost track of the numerous rules and lessons of love that Shams preaches. Throughout the last half of the book Shams’ character is breaking many of the rules the author places earlier in the book and the whole “Forty Rules of Love” becomes a severely disjointed and amateur effort.<br /><br />Honestly, it seems like the author failed to stick to the principal theme of this book in the latter half of the book and even the characters felt slipping from their original intent whatsoever and in the end the book is just all about American Jewish housewife who finds love with a bohemian Sufi mystic ...tells intertwined stories separated by centuries... Nor does she convey any sense of religious profundity or transcendence. <br /><br />As per all the "Forty Rules of Love" are another set of another quotidian highlight-worthy rules.<br /><br />Another thing apart from the all the good quotes is that I got to know the reason behind Rumi turning into this legendary poet, which was because of Shams of Tabriz, his lover ( spiritual lover ).<br /><br />I had really high expectations but it all fell flat just like this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2951526947896099510" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1300548102" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1300548102" />
    <a title="Nabilah Firdaus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28134808-nabilah-firdaus"><img alt="Nabilah Firdaus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438020137p2/28134808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1300548102?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nabilah Firdaus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nabilah Firdaus" href="/user/show/28134808-nabilah-firdaus">Nabilah Firdaus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28134808-nabilah-firdaus?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28134808-nabilah-firdaus?shelf=middle-eastern-literature">middle-eastern-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28134808-nabilah-firdaus?shelf=2015-reading-challenge">2015-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1300548102" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2637946722784499759">Okay, where do I start with a book like this? I’m scared my review wont do the book justice, because this book is a beauty, devastatingly beautiful.<br/><br/><br/>The Forty Rules of Love consists of two parallel narratives. The first narrative is about Ella, who works for a literary agency and she was given a book entitled Sweet Blasphemy and yep, the Sweet Blasphemy is the second narrative of this novel. It’s a story of the great mystic Sufi, Jalaluddin Rumi with his soul instructor, disciple, companion and a</span>
  <span id="freeText2637946722784499759" style="display:none">Okay, where do I start with a book like this? I’m scared my review wont do the book justice, because this book is a beauty, devastatingly beautiful.<br /><br /><br />The Forty Rules of Love consists of two parallel narratives. The first narrative is about Ella, who works for a literary agency and she was given a book entitled Sweet Blasphemy and yep, the Sweet Blasphemy is the second narrative of this novel. It’s a story of the great mystic Sufi, Jalaluddin Rumi with his soul instructor, disciple, companion and a soulmate, Shams of Tabriz in their quest for eternal love of God. <br /><br />The first narrative of Ella was somehow odd and empty, I didn’t find it exciting as the second one. Shafak really nailed in in narrating the second narrative, the Sweet Blasphemy. The way it was shown from many perspectives, from Shams perspective, the beggar, Sulaiman the Drunk, Desert Rose the Harlot, and Rumi;s family was really captivating.<br /><br /><br />The crust of the story is about yes, the old and frequently written theme – love – but not the typical cheesy and mushy stuffs of love, it is something different, a widenened concept of love. <br /><br /><br />I loved everything about this book and most of it is because of Shams of Tabriz, from his exquisite rules of love, to his bold personality, to his love and belief in God, EVERYTHING ABOUT HIM WAS SO BEAUTIFUL AND MESMERIZING. This is the first time that I have so much respect for a book character. Nak fangirling pun macam serba salah sebab hormat, kah kah kah. This is definitely one of the best books I have ever read. The narratives, the characters, the plot twists, the 40 rules themselves, are a marvelous piece of wisdom and inspiration. <br /><br />This is a must read for everyone. A book that goes beyond all faiths, beliefs, thoughts and current societal conditions. Highly recommended, baca tau.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2637946722784499759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5428014378344555734">Ella Rubenstein keeps busy enough cooking and caring for her husband and children and their big Victorian house in Northampton, Massachusetts, that she can mostly ignore her anger at her husband's infidelities and her sense of loss at the children's lessening need for her as they focus on high school and college. Chance provides her an opportunity to rekindle her own college-age passion for literature as a reader for a Boston publishing house.<br/><br/>We read her first assignment, <i>Sweet Blasphemy</i>, by A. </span>
  <span id="freeText5428014378344555734" style="display:none">Ella Rubenstein keeps busy enough cooking and caring for her husband and children and their big Victorian house in Northampton, Massachusetts, that she can mostly ignore her anger at her husband's infidelities and her sense of loss at the children's lessening need for her as they focus on high school and college. Chance provides her an opportunity to rekindle her own college-age passion for literature as a reader for a Boston publishing house.<br /><br />We read her first assignment, <i>Sweet Blasphemy</i>, by A. Z. Zahara of Holland, along with her, and learn the story of the intense friendship of the Sufi dervish Shams of Tabriz and the Islamic scholar Rumi, in thirteenth-century Turkey. Reading the manuscript inspires Ella to connect with its author via the Internet, a step that takes her life into a whole new direction, as Rumi's experience with Shams transforms him into a great mystic and poet.<br /><br />The fictionalized account of the real-life Rumi and Shams is stronger than the contemporary branch of this dual timeline novel, and I would have liked a more interesting denouement, but I enjoyed the novel and learned a lot about a place and time with which I was unfamiliar.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_800188707" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/800188707" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875291-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565352267p2/5875291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/800188707?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/5875291-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=islam-sufism">islam-sufism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer800188707" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12095279495425757464">I cannot think of a book that has spoken to me more directly in a particular period of time. The "Rules of the Religion of Love" articulated by Shams state universal truths that we all realize deep down, but it is the way that the message is put together that really penetrates to our core essence. <br/><br/>The Sufi path is one of getting to the meaning of things. It always desires to go deeper to experience ultimate reality. This requires losing conceptions of God, religion, relationships, life, etc... Y</span>
  <span id="freeText12095279495425757464" style="display:none">I cannot think of a book that has spoken to me more directly in a particular period of time. The "Rules of the Religion of Love" articulated by Shams state universal truths that we all realize deep down, but it is the way that the message is put together that really penetrates to our core essence. <br /><br />The Sufi path is one of getting to the meaning of things. It always desires to go deeper to experience ultimate reality. This requires losing conceptions of God, religion, relationships, life, etc... Yet there is a tension in this in that the Sufis do not say to discard a particular path. They are simply more concerned with the meaning behind it. It is like losing the desire for idle conversation and pleasantries. Even conceptions of "God" are "idle conversation" in many instances. God is unknowable, yet also knowable through certain universal truths that reside inside. Therefore the Sufi finds companions in all people who live by truths such as equality, justice, compassion and most importantly love.<br /> <br />I do desire to walk the Sufi path, and I found myself relating to the character Aziz in this book. His past and mine were nearly identical, from losing a wife to death to the experience with a debauched lifestyle. The restlessness of his nomadic drive to journey physically and discover also resonates with my own decision to move to the Middle East, a decision that I still cannot articulate in a truly profound fashion. Some meanings are beyond words. That may also be why my desire to write has diminished as of late. Experiences seem to be replacing that desire. <br /> <br />In the Islamic tradition, two of the names of God are "The All-Forgiving" and "The All-Compassionate". Along these lines, Shams emphasizes that God is not sitting around waiting to destroy us at every turn. After all, we are manifestations of God, and God is not "out there" just as much as God is not "in here". This realization of the limits of knowledge requires a loving attitude to life and others, as well as a forgiving attitude. The unknowable makes us question how we truly can know outside of the principles of love and humility. It should put a check on aggression, violence and oppression in all religious circles. Yet when we grasp onto the externalities of a dualistic world, we will always see relative good and relative evil and believe both to be real rather than recognizing The ONE that runs through it all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12095279495425757464" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11576021942103373825" style="display:none">I should define this book as a spiritual journey actually. I won’t say it was astounding, but I also won’t be saying it was something petty! <br /><br />As a lover of words, I loved the book. On the contrary, as a reader, I didn’t enjoy it to that extent. It bored me a bit as all the talking on Sufism was getting really intense. <br /><br />I face problem in reading books like this where each chapter starts with different characters. I feel the flow of my reading somehow breaks there. <br /><br />Besides, in this Sufism theme, the character Ella felt a little inappropriate to me. Whenever I was trying to concentrate on the spiritual facts, Ella appeared with her family problems! It was annoying. <br /><br />I knew Rumi. I read Rumi. His words content my soul. But Shams of Tabriz was a new name to me. His forty rules of love amazed me. I would be preserving this book for the quotes for sure. <br /><br />Let me finish the review with one of the forty rules which I loved the most- <br /><br /><i> <b>“Loneliness and solitude are two different things. When you are lonely, it is easy to delude yourself into believing that you are on the right path. Solitude is better for us, as it means being alone without feeling lonely. But eventually it is the best to find a person who will be your mirror. Remember only in another person’s heart can you truly see yourself and the presence of God within you.”</b></i> </span>

<div id="review_2200179112" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2200179112" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2200179112?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2200179112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16462879977644915381">As one of the characters suggests, there is a central dichotomy within organised religions between the spiritual and intellectual, between orthodoxy and originality, with individuals teetering on the brink between prophet and mad-man, a herald and a heretic, between a Mohammed and a Shabs, one revered by millions of followers and the other murdered, their corpse dumped in a well, an irrelevant footnote in history. Another strange, almost contradictory, aspect to religion, is how, once it's dogma</span>
  <span id="freeText16462879977644915381" style="display:none">As one of the characters suggests, there is a central dichotomy within organised religions between the spiritual and intellectual, between orthodoxy and originality, with individuals teetering on the brink between prophet and mad-man, a herald and a heretic, between a Mohammed and a Shabs, one revered by millions of followers and the other murdered, their corpse dumped in a well, an irrelevant footnote in history. Another strange, almost contradictory, aspect to religion, is how, once it's dogmas are established and embedded, it considers any new or original views or personalities to be heretic, without realising that, at one point, their own views were considered unorthodox and revolutionary.<br /><br />'Forty Rules of Love' explores the meeting of two, great original minds; the poet Rumi and the dervish Shams. Shams teachers Rumi, at the time a great scholar and intellectual to slowly let go and discard all the things that he held precious; his fame, money, prestige and intellectual reputation, the social norms and religious orthodoxies which formed a key part of his beliefs, to become an artist and the poet he was destined to become. Subsumed within this is the Sufi views which Shams espouses; of religious tolerance, of mysticism and open-mindedness and the importance of individuals finding their own way to go. Islam is too often-and unjustly-castigated as being a parochial religion of fundamentalism, ignoring the fact that Islam was essential in spreading and encouraging intellectual enquiry and creating art-whilst it is true a streak of fundamentalism runs through Islam, was with any religion or belief, it would be silly to ignore the fact that for many hundreds of years it was synonymous with being forward thinking and has been responsible for creating and maintaining many of the most important philosophical, scientific and political ideas in civilisation.<br /><br />But I digress. 'The Forty Rules of Love' is a wonderful piece of meta-fiction; of a fiction, within a fiction with another fiction. The other two central characters are Ella, a literary reviewer who has lived an emotionally and spiritually unfulfilled life and the wandering Scottish Sufi, Aziz. Ella is reviewing Aziz's story about Rumi and Shams, 'Sweet Blasphemy', written by the itinerant  mystic Aziz. Ella, repressed and taciturn, stands in contrast to the broad-minded and ebullient Azia-in many ways their relationship is analogous to Rumi and Shams, two souls brought together via love. The depiction of Ella and Azia is, at times, slightly cloying and cliched, their characters representing more ideas about love, life and religion Shafak is conveying that fully realised characters.  However the real strength of the novel is the multiple narratives which Shafak interweaves in 'Sweet Blasphemy', from the devout fundamentalist Sheikh Yassin , to the down-trodden prostitute Desert Rose, the characters are full-realised, each on a journey to find meaning in a world which will so often deny any exists. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16462879977644915381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771099171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9132191613492124548"><em><strong>The Forty Rules of Love has been on my wish list for a long time, and I wish I had got to it sooner. I instantly fell in love with it!</strong> </em>Although, when I think about it, maybe it's best to read books when you've forgotten even the blurb. This is how I picked up the forty Rules of Love – a quick glance through the shelf in the library, me remembering I wanted to read this years ago, considering I must've had a good reason, and here we are. <em>
  <strong>And like I said, me and the book instantly clicked!</strong>
</em> <br/><br/><em><strong>It’</strong></em></span>
  <span id="freeText9132191613492124548" style="display:none"><em><strong>The Forty Rules of Love has been on my wish list for a long time, and I wish I had got to it sooner. I instantly fell in love with it!</strong> </em>Although, when I think about it, maybe it's best to read books when you've forgotten even the blurb. This is how I picked up the forty Rules of Love – a quick glance through the shelf in the library, me remembering I wanted to read this years ago, considering I must've had a good reason, and here we are. <em>
  <strong>And like I said, me and the book instantly clicked!</strong>
</em> <br><br><em><strong>It’s a surprisingly poetic book, and yet – it’s also really simple to follow.</strong> </em>The writing and emotions feel sincere, the language, while being aesthetically pleasing, doesn't seem pretentious at all. And perhaps the thing I liked the most is the fantastic element of an ancient, outlandish story intertwining with the modern world.<em><strong> I feel like this is a book that could appeal both to hard-core fans of books like the Alchemist, and also to people who, um, like simpler, less flowery prose and maybe less dreamy sounding slogans for spirituality.</strong> </em>Why "um"? Because I liked The Alchemist too when I was 16, but a little while later I started thinking that maybe it was a much too sweet and sterilized view of spirituality for me. So I don't want to offend the fans, because I also understand why they like it, but I find myself somewhere in the middle of the two crowds, when it comes to the <em>"dreamy"</em> kind of spirituality like that. Anyway, what I'm saying is that <em>
  <strong>The Forty Rules of Love has a little bit of that same feel in the story – enough so that the fans of the Alchemist might enjoy it, but also not enough to make the others roll their eyes. So basically, just the right amount!</strong>
</em><br><br>Come read all of the reasons why I loved and can recommend this book – the full review is <a href="http://avalinahsbooks.space/forty-rules-of-love-elif-shafak/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">here, on my book blog</a>.<br><br><i>Triggers: Shame on me, because I didn't write the triggers down a month ago, when I read this book. So I'll try to remember to the best of my ability! From what I can remember, there's <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[persecution over your religious beliefs, even if it's in the confines of the same religion, dissolution of marriage, death by cancer, losing a loved one, death by murder, violence and rape. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></i><br><br><a href="http://avalinahsbooks.space/forty-rules-of-love-elif-shafak/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206431134" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Fennelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2470827-jeffrey-fennelly"><img alt="Jeffrey Fennelly" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/206431134?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Fennelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Fennelly" href="/user/show/2470827-jeffrey-fennelly">Jeffrey Fennelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206431134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17574932981568547840">PLEASE APPROACH THIS BOOK WITH CAUTION: if you have standards for style, you will be greatly disappointed. I gave it an extra star only because it taught me a good deal about a culture about which I could claim scant knowledge before reading the book. I found it consistently confounding, a potentially compelling story ruined by weak writing. It seems that while crafting it, the author was either careless, distracted, lazy, or some combination of these. It is painfully loaded with clichés, to a d</span>
  <span id="freeText17574932981568547840" style="display:none">PLEASE APPROACH THIS BOOK WITH CAUTION: if you have standards for style, you will be greatly disappointed. I gave it an extra star only because it taught me a good deal about a culture about which I could claim scant knowledge before reading the book. I found it consistently confounding, a potentially compelling story ruined by weak writing. It seems that while crafting it, the author was either careless, distracted, lazy, or some combination of these. It is painfully loaded with clichés, to a degree made surprising by Shafak's previous accolades. The New Republic's review is perhaps the most accurate I've read, and it struck me as an almost verbatim repeat of my reaction to the book. The most shocking revelation of this review was that Shafak so carefully translated the book herself, going back and forth between Turkish and English to hone its language. It seems she ignored her original, painfully weak choices in the process. This book was a colossal disappointment. Here's the TNR review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.tnr.com/book/review/lost-translation">http://www.tnr.com/book/review/lost-t...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17574932981568547840" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1513300870" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1513300870" />
    <a title="Faroukh Naseem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47239298-faroukh-naseem"><img alt="Faroukh Naseem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588328903p2/47239298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1513300870?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Faroukh Naseem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Faroukh Naseem" href="/user/show/47239298-faroukh-naseem">Faroukh Naseem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241972939" href="/book/show/25701206-the-forty-rules-of-love">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1513300870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7234205077288146185">I didn't like this book, at all. Not really, I did enjoy the first 70 odd pages and actually thought it could be one of my favorites.<br/>‎<br/>Something in the book was fundamentally wrong. What troubled me most was that on hand there are religious‎ statements and on the other hand obscenely descriptive 'adult' scenes. It's how it's become ok to curse using Jesus Christ's name. (if you know what I'm saying) There is something ethically wrong in that and you just can't do that.<br/><br/>Having said that, it's ficti</span>
  <span id="freeText7234205077288146185" style="display:none">I didn't like this book, at all. Not really, I did enjoy the first 70 odd pages and actually thought it could be one of my favorites.<br />‎<br />Something in the book was fundamentally wrong. What troubled me most was that on hand there are religious‎ statements and on the other hand obscenely descriptive 'adult' scenes. It's how it's become ok to curse using Jesus Christ's name. (if you know what I'm saying) There is something ethically wrong in that and you just can't do that.<br /><br />Having said that, it's fiction so we need to 'judge' it accordingly. The writing was very bland and somewhere these 'rules' became almost a burden, maybe even for Elif Shafak to incorporate in the story.<br /><br />You see references to each and every rule almost forced into the story. Some thing will be going on and suddenly Shams of Tabriz or some other character will say "it is on of the rules" "rule number 32 say" "that is one of the rules" I wish that we would be treated smart enough to know we would understand that the reference is made to the rules.<br /><br />You flow across 10+ characters and each time it's a first person account which was very smart but at the same time, in my opinion, there should be a distinct change in the writing so you don't have to 'think' that it's The Killer, or 'Suleiman the drunk' or 'Rumi' or 'Aladdin' or 'Desert Rose'. It seemed like every character had the same lexicon and same way of talking thinking/dreaming.<br /><br />***Spoiler Alert***‎<br /><br />Why Ella does what she does is absolutely impractical. I'm not talking about Morals. I'm talking about it being so strange and unbelievable that a woman would fall in love and decide to leave her family (her husband may have deserved it,but her children didn't) And what really pissed me off, is the root cause of all this was someone who believed in 'Love' and the power and Importance of Love. A person who is out there to spread love in the world without prejudice and all that jazz is the root cause of a broken family? He has an affair with a married woman? When at a point he had the chance to let her go and leave, he still choses her and makes her believe that she is the one who is chosing him. There are a lot of things I can't fathom about this book and it's characters.<br /><br />In the end I'm left thinking only one thing? How can someone wanting to spread so much love, leave so many broken hearts?</span>
  <a data-text-id="7234205077288146185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1779538378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1779538378" />
    <a title="Sophia Triad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44642860-sophia-triad"><img alt="Sophia Triad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591419108p2/44642860.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1779538378?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sophia Triad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sophia Triad" href="/user/show/44642860-sophia-triad">Sophia Triad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141047188" href="/book/show/10452283-the-forty-rules-of-love">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44642860-sophia-triad?shelf=2contemporary-lovestories">2contemporary-lovestories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44642860-sophia-triad?shelf=5historical-lovestories-oldtimes">5historical-lovestories-oldtimes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1779538378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3689102241568608188">Philosophy, poetry and forbidden love, which comes a bit too late<br/><br/>The main heroine of this book made me so angry! <br/><br/>Ella is forty, she is married with three grown up children, she lives in a beautiful house with a beautiful garden, she has just got a part time job as a reader for a literally agency and she is utterly unhappy.<br/><br/>The question is “was she ever happy?” She married her husband at a young age. I am not sure if she ever loved him or if she stopped loving him as soon as the children were born</span>
  <span id="freeText3689102241568608188" style="display:none">Philosophy, poetry and forbidden love, which comes a bit too late<br /><br />The main heroine of this book made me so angry! <br /><br />Ella is forty, she is married with three grown up children, she lives in a beautiful house with a beautiful garden, she has just got a part time job as a reader for a literally agency and she is utterly unhappy.<br /><br />The question is “was she ever happy?” She married her husband at a young age. I am not sure if she ever loved him or if she stopped loving him as soon as the children were born and she needed to shift her attention and her love to them. She was tolerating her husband’s infidelities without saying a word. I suppose her husband has had numerous flings. He even got a letter from a hotel he was using, in which they asked him if he was happy with the services provided!<br /><br />And the heroine was just standing there in the middle of this beautiful house, in the middle of this “beautiful” life being surrounded by her family and friends and feeling completely alone. She basically was living her life like it was a movie and she has just there to play the part. Like a ghost, like a zombie. <br /><br />I guess in cases like this she could kill herself truly or metaphorically speaking. Or she could snap. And that is essentially what she does. She snaps. While reading a manuscript about the 13th century Sufi poet Rumi and Shams, she starts an exchange of emails with the mysterious author of the book, Aziz. The manuscript is talking about love and rules about love. <br /><br />Ella suddenly awakens and starts wondering if she loves, if she ever loved and if she ever been loved. Her relationship with the author is on the whole through exchange of emails. <br /><br />When Ella and Aziz finally meet, it is glorious but it is short-lived. <br /><br />This book made me so angry! And it made me shedding tears in the end.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3689102241568608188" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fAyWRyfkJaeZmlYS1C4CSE/dyAxw4k5BK/d3W3ZEBPVGKv+VnLrszz6x4nMj2wiXmYIvxL5vwCZwiTHquwf2bw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uTtZVDMsVdtkZ0m5D8c5We/9f8Gx0i7BhdnD2nnOfm+DHTCGiHKcs8NM/dj4MjOGOaKYCX9foKbep4VrtI2M9Q==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Not much is known about his life except that he lived in thirteenth-century Anatolia (now Turkey), had a great spiritual friendship with a wild man called Shams, brought an adopted daughter into his family, and was distraught when Shams finally disappeared.\n\nRumi\'s Daughter is the delightful novel about Kimya, the girl who was sent from her rural village to live in Rumi\'s home. She already had mystical tendencies, and learned a great deal under Rumi\'s tutelage. Eventually she married Shams, an unusual husband, almost totally absorbed by his longings for God. Their marriage was fiery and different and, in the end, dissolved by Kimya\'s death - after which Shams vanished.\n\nRumi\'s Daughter tells Kimya\'s story with great charm and tenderness. Well written and thought-provoking, it is sure to draw comparison with Paolho Coelho\'s The Alchemist, and also to add something fresh and new to what is so far known about Rumi.<\/span>\n  <a که مقصود نویسنده کتاب «هن<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6512227944170188873\" style=\"display:none\">خاویر کرمنت نویسنده کتاب بیشعوری 26 سال پیش نسبت به شخصیت دونالد ترامپ هشدار می‌دهد و به طور آشکار او را بیشعور می‌خواند. در جایی از کتاب بیشعوری به کتاب مقدس بیشعورهای تاجر به نام «هنر دزدی» اثر «دونالد کرامپ» اشاره می‌شود. که مقصود نویسنده کتاب «هنر معامله» نوشته «دونالد ترامپ»، رئیس جمهور فعلی آمریکا، است.\nدر کتاب می شنویم:\n«کتاب مقدس این نوع بیشعورها (بیشعورهای تاجر) کتابی است به نام هنر دزدی از دونالد کرامپ که می‌نویسد:«راستی و درستی، مهمل و بی‌معنی اند. همیشه مفیدترین راهکارها در تجارت، مدیریت درستکاری عداد و ارقام است. چرا باید برای به‌دست آوردن چیزی به سختی و با صداقت کار کرد وقتی می‌توان آن را به راحتی دزدید؟»\nچاره‌ای هم نیست وقتی یک نفر با کمبود لیاقت و توانایی اصرار داشته باشد که در جلسات، مذاکرات و محاسبات به هر نحوی کارش را پیش ببرد، باید کمبودهایش را با حقه بازی، حرافی و بلوف زدن جبران کند. بیشعورهای گنده‌گو از همینجا پیدا می‌شوند. او هنرنمایی یک گنده‌گو وقتی است که سرنخ کلام از دستش رفته باشد و نداند درباره چی حرف می‌زند. چنین بیشعوری خودش را در برانگیختن دیگران (چرندگویی)، خلاقیت(دزدیدن ایده‌های دیگران)، تعامل (ارعاب دیگران) و مدیریت (سرپوش گذاشتن بر گندکاری‌هایش) خبره نشان می‌دهد.\nکتاب صوتی بیشعوری اثر خاویر کرمنت است. نویسنده ای که بیست سال آزگار با بیشعوری خود، خانواده و اطرافیانش را آزار داد. از دید او تقریبا همه افراد بیشعورند. اگر معتقدید که بهتر است کرمنت درمورد زندگی خودش نظر بدهد باید بگویم بی شعوری یک بیماری است که اغلب افراد مبتلا، از آن بی اطلاع هستند و معمولا در قبال این اتهام می گویند: من بي‌شعور نيستم، تو بیشعوري. بعضي خصوصيات مشترك بیشعورها از اين قرار است: خودپسندي فجيع، نفرت انگيزي بي حد، خيرخواهي متكبرانه، ضميرناخودآگاه غيرقابل نفوذ، كسب قدرت با خوار و ضعيف كردن ديگران، امتنا از صفات اصلي انساني و سوءاستفاده بي رحمانه از آدم های ساده. اگر هنوز هم فکر می کنید که تنها همسایه بی ملاحظه یا مدیر بی احساستان گرفتار بیشعوری هستند پیشنهاد می کنم این کتاب را گوش کنید تا با مثال های ملموس خاویر کرمنت مصداق های بارز بیشعوری را در خود و اطرافتان ببینید.\n\nاین کتاب در سال 1990 در امریکا منتشر شد و محمود فرجامی آن را اولین بار در سال 1390 به زبان فارسی ترجمه نمود. هرچند به دلیل نگرفتن مجوز چاپ آن را به صورت اینترنتی منتشر کرد تا بالاخره در سال 1394 به صورت چاپی در ویترین کتاب فروشی ها قرار گرفت. این کتاب در ایران از همان ابتدا با استقبال فراوانی مواجه شد که البته نثر روان و طنزآلود فرجامی در این محبوبیت بی تأثیر نبوده است.\n\nبیشعوری نوعی اعتیاد است مثل اعتیاد به مواد مخدر یا الکل. فرد معتاد تمایل به تکرار کار خود دارد و این اعتیاد از فرد به جامعه سرایت می کند و محیطی آزاردهنده را به وجود می آورد. بیشعورها چندین دسته اند و ویژگی های مشخصی دارند. آن ها قانون های نانوشته ای دارند، مثلا: تمام قوانين براي اين به وجود آمده‌اند كه نقض شوند، اما فقط تو، حق داري كه آن را زيرپا بگذاري نه ديگران و یا تمام مشكلات را ديگران به وجود آورده‌اند. آن ها بر این باورند که علت اعتراض مهم نيست. مهم خود اعتراض كردن است كه به خاطر آن زندگي مي کنند و همواره معترضند.\n\nکمترین بهره ای که پس از شنیدن کتاب می برید، این است که می توانید بیشعورها را بشناسید و از آن ها دوری کنید اما، شاید آنچه که در این کتاب صوتی شنونده را بیش از یک کتاب طنز و یا بیانگر واقعیت های تلخ اجتماعی با خود همراه می کند نشان دادن روش برخورد با بیشعورها و چگونگی درمان بیشعوری است. خاویر کرمنت انواع و اقسام مثال ها از انسان های بیشعور را با ذکر جزئی ترین رفتارها بیان می کند و چون این فضا قصه های واقعی از زندگی خود و نزدیکانش را به همراه دارد شیرینی دوچندانی به کتاب می بخشد.\n\nهرکسی گفته هیچ بیشعوری در دنیا وجود ندارد، خودش هم بیشعور است. من سه تا بچه بیشعور دارم و هر روز دارم هر چهارتاشان را تحمل می کنم. آخر شوهرم هم بیشعور است.\n- خاویر کرمنت؛       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6542440.Elif_Shafak">About Elif Shafak</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6542440.Elif_Shafak"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1395322919p3/6542440.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/6542440.Elif_Shafak">Elif Shafak

<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15860528254455961865">Elif Shafak is an award-winning British-Turkish novelist and the most widely read female author in Turkey. She writes in both Turkish and English, and has published seventeen books, eleven of which are novels. Her work has been translated into fifty languages. Shafak holds a PhD in political science and she has taught at various universities in Turkey, the US and the UK, including St Anne's Colleg</span>
  <span id="freeText15860528254455961865" style="display:none">Elif Shafak is an award-winning British-Turkish novelist and the most widely read female author in Turkey. She writes in both Turkish and English, and has published seventeen books, eleven of which are novels. Her work has been translated into fifty languages. Shafak holds a PhD in political science and she has taught at various universities in Turkey, the US and the UK, including St Anne's College, Oxford University, where she is an honorary fellow. She is a member of Weforum Global Agenda Council on Creative Economy and a founding member of ECFR (European Council on Foreign Relations). An advocate for women's rights, LGBT rights and freedom of speech, Shafak is an inspiring public speaker and twice a TED Global speaker, each time receiving a standing ovation. Shafak contributes to major publications around the world and she has been awarded the title of Chevalier des Arts et des Lettres. In 2017 she was chosen by Politico as one of the twelve people who would make the world better. She has judged numerous literary prizes and is chairing the Wellcome Prize 2019. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.elifshafak.com">www.elifshafak.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15860528254455961865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
